This client also interacts with the broker to allow groups of\\n57:  * consumers to load balance consumption using <a href=\\\"#consumergroups\\\">consumer groups</a>.\\n58:  * <p>\\n59:  * The consumer maintains TCP connections to the necessary brokers to fetch data.\\n60:  * Failure to close the consumer after use will leak these connections.\\n61:  * The consumer is not thread-safe. See <a href=\\\"#multithreaded\\\">Multi-threaded Processing</a> for more details.\\n62:  *\\n63:  * <h3>Cross-Version Compatibility</h3>\\n64:  * This client can communicate with brokers that are version 0.10.0 or newer. Older or newer brokers may not support\\n65:  * certain features. For example, 0.10.0 brokers do not support offsetsForTimes, because this feature was added\\n66:  * in version 0.10.1. You will receive an {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.UnsupportedVersionException}\\n67:  * when invoking an API that is not available on the running broker version.\\n68:  * <p>\\n69:  *\\n70:  * <h3>Offsets and Consumer Position</h3>\\n71:  * Kafka maintains a numerical offset for each record in a partition. This offset acts as a unique identifier of\\n72:  * a record within that partition, and also denotes the position of the consumer in the partition. For example, a consumer\\n73:  * which is at position 5 has consumed records with offsets 0 through 4 and will next receive the record with offset 5.\\n74:  * Note that offsets are not guaranteed to be consecutive (such as compacted topic or when records have been produced\\n75:  * using transactions). For example, if the consumer did read a record with offset 4, but 5 is not an offset\\n76:  * with a record, its position might advance to 6 (or higher) directly. Similarly, if the consumer's position is 5,\\n77:  * but there is no record with offset 5, the consumer will return the record with the next higher offset.\\n78:  * There are actually two notions of position relevant to the user of the consumer:\\n79:  * <p>\\n80:  * The {@link #position(TopicPartition) position} of the consumer gives the offset of the next record that will be given\\n81:  * out. It will be one larger than the highest offset the consumer has seen in that partition. It automatically advances\\n82:  * every time the consumer receives messages in a call to {@link #poll(Duration)}.\\n83:  * <p>\\n84:  * The {@link #commitSync() committed position} is the last offset that has been stored securely. Should the\\n85:  * process fail and restart, this is the offset that the consumer will recover to. The consumer can either automatically commit\\n86:  * offsets periodically; or it can choose to control this committed position manually by calling one of the commit APIs\\n87:  * (e.g. {@link #commitSync() commitSync} and {@link #commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback) commitAsync}).\\n88:  * <p>\\n89:  * This distinction gives the consumer control over when a record is considered consumed. It is discussed in further\\n90:  * detail below.\\n91:  *\\n92:  * <h3><a name=\\\"consumergroups\\\">Consumer Groups and Topic Subscriptions</a></h3>\\n93:  *\\n94:  * Kafka uses the concept of <i>consumer groups</i> to allow a pool of processes to divide the work of consuming and\\n95:  * processing records. These processes can either be running on the same machine or they can be\\n96:  * distributed over many machines to provide scalability and fault tolerance for processing. All consumer instances\\n97:  * sharing the same {@code group.id} will be part of the same consumer group.\\n98:  * <p>\\n99:  * Each consumer in a group can dynamically set the list of topics it wants to subscribe to through one of the\\n100:  * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribe} APIs. import java.util.regex.Pattern;\n49: \n50: import static org.apache.kafka.common.utils.Utils.propsToMap;\n51: \n52: /**\n53:  * A client that consumes records from a Kafka cluster.\n54:  * <p>\n55:  * This client transparently handles the failure of Kafka brokers, and transparently adapts as topic partitions\n56:  * it fetches migrate within the cluster. This client also interacts with the broker to allow groups of\n57:  * consumers to load balance consumption using <a href=\"#consumergroups\">consumer groups</a>.\n58:  * <p>\n59:  * The consumer maintains TCP connections to the necessary brokers to fetch data.\n60:  * Failure to close the consumer after use will leak these connections.\n61:  * The consumer is not thread-safe. See <a href=\"#multithreaded\">Multi-threaded Processing</a> for more details.\n62:  *\n63:  * <h3>Cross-Version Compatibility</h3>\n64:  * This client can communicate with brokers that are version 0.10.0 or newer. Older or newer brokers may not support\n65:  * certain features. For example, 0.10.0 brokers do not support offsetsForTimes, because this feature was added\n66:  * in version 0.10.1. You will receive an {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.UnsupportedVersionException}\n67:  * when invoking an API that is not available on the running broker version.\n68:  * <p>\n69:  *\n70:  * <h3>Offsets and Consumer Position</h3>\n71:  * Kafka maintains a numerical offset for each record in a partition. This offset acts as a unique identifier of\n72:  * a record within that partition, and also denotes the position of the consumer in the partition. For example, a consumer\n73:  * which is at position 5 has consumed records with offsets 0 through 4 and will next receive the record with offset 5.\n74:  * Note that offsets are not guaranteed to be consecutive (such as compacted topic or when records have been produced\n75:  * using transactions). For example, if the consumer did read a record with offset 4, but 5 is not an offset\n76:  * with a record, its position might advance to 6 (or higher) directly. Similarly, if the consumer's position is 5,\n77:  * but there is no record with offset 5, the consumer will return the record with the next higher offset.\n78:  * There are actually two notions of position relevant to the user of the consumer:\n79:  * <p>\n80:  * The {@link #position(TopicPartition) position} of the consumer gives the offset of the next record that will be given\n81:  * out. It will be one larger than the highest offset the consumer has seen in that partition. It automatically advances\n82:  * every time the consumer receives messages in a call to {@link #poll(Duration)}.\n83:  * <p>\n84:  * The {@link #commitSync() committed position} is the last offset that has been stored securely. Should the\n85:  * process fail and restart, this is the offset that the consumer will recover to. The consumer can either automatically commit\n86:  * offsets periodically; or it can choose to control this committed position manually by calling one of the commit APIs\n87:  * (e.g. {@link #commitSync() commitSync} and {@link #commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback) commitAsync}).\n88:  * <p>\n89:  * This distinction gives the consumer control over when a record is considered consumed. It is discussed in further\n90:  * detail below.\n91:  *\n92:  * <h3><a name=\"consumergroups\">Consumer Groups and Topic Subscriptions</a></h3>\n93:  *\n94:  * Kafka uses the concept of <i>consumer groups</i> to allow a pool of processes to divide the work of consuming and\n95:  * processing records. These processes can either be running on the same machine or they can be\n96:  * distributed over many machines to provide scalability and fault tolerance for processing. All consumer instances\n97:  * sharing the same {@code group.id} will be part of the same consumer group.\n98:  * <p>\n99:  * Each consumer in a group can dynamically set the list of topics it wants to subscribe to through one of the\n100:  * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribe} APIs. {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_015qUVkTLXTJtnpCW2uSQ5kG","type":"tool_result","content":"{\"content\":\"100:  * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribe} APIs. Kafka will deliver each message in the\\n101:  * subscribed topics to one process in each consumer group. This is achieved by balancing the partitions between all\\n102:  * members in the consumer group so that each partition is assigned to exactly one consumer in the group. So if there\\n103:  * is a topic with four partitions, and a consumer group with two processes, each process would consume from two partitions.\\n104:  * <p>\\n105:  * Membership in a consumer group is maintained dynamically: if a process fails, the partitions assigned to it will\\n106:  * be reassigned to other consumers in the same group. Similarly, if a new consumer joins the group, partitions will be moved\\n107:  * from existing consumers to the new one. This is known as <i>rebalancing</i> the group and is discussed in more\\n108:  * detail <a href=\\\"#failuredetection\\\">below</a>. Group rebalancing is also used when new partitions are added\\n109:  * to one of the subscribed topics or when a new topic matching a {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribed regex}\\n110:  * is created. The group will automatically detect the new partitions through periodic metadata refreshes and\\n111:  * assign them to members of the group.\\n112:  * <p>\\n113:  * Conceptually you can think of a consumer group as being a single logical subscriber that happens to be made up of\\n114:  * multiple processes. As a multi-subscriber system, Kafka naturally supports having any number of consumer groups for a\\n115:  * given topic without duplicating data (additional consumers are actually quite cheap).\\n116:  * <p>\\n117:  * This is a slight generalization of the functionality that is common in messaging systems. To get semantics similar to\\n118:  * a queue in a traditional messaging system all processes would be part of a single consumer group and hence record\\n119:  * delivery would be balanced over the group like with a queue. Unlike a traditional messaging system, though, you can\\n120:  * have multiple such groups. To get semantics similar to pub-sub in a traditional messaging system each process would\\n121:  * have its own consumer group, so each process would subscribe to all the records published to the topic.\\n122:  * <p>\\n123:  * In addition, when group reassignment happens automatically, consumers can be notified through a {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener},\\n124:  * which allows them to finish necessary application-level logic such as state cleanup, manual offset\\n125:  * commits, etc. See <a href=\\\"#rebalancecallback\\\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</a> for more details.\\n126:  * <p>\\n127:  * It is also possible for the consumer to <a href=\\\"#manualassignment\\\">manually assign</a> specific partitions\\n128:  * (similar to the older \\\"simple\\\" consumer) using {@link #assign(Collection)}. In this case, dynamic partition\\n129:  * assignment and consumer group coordination will be disabled.\\n130:  *\\n131:  * <h3><a name=\\\"failuredetection\\\">Detecting Consumer Failures</a></h3>\\n132:  *\\n133:  * After subscribing to a set of topics, the consumer will automatically join the group when {@link #poll(Duration)} is\\n134:  * invoked. The poll API is designed to ensure consumer liveness. As long as you continue to call poll, the consumer\\n135:  * will stay in the group and continue to receive messages from the partitions it was assigned. Underneath the covers,\\n136:  * the consumer sends periodic heartbeats to the server. If the consumer crashes or is unable to send heartbeats for\\n137:  * a duration of {@code session.timeout.ms}, then the consumer will be considered dead and its partitions will\\n138:  * be reassigned.\\n139:  * <p>\\n140:  * It is also possible that the consumer could encounter a \\\"livelock\\\" situation where it is continuing\\n141:  * to send heartbeats, but no progress is being made. To prevent the consumer from holding onto its partitions\\n142:  * indefinitely in this case, we provide a liveness detection mechanism using the {@code max.poll.interval.ms}\\n143:  * setting. Basically if you don't call poll at least as frequently as the configured max interval,\\n144:  * then the client will proactively leave the group so that another consumer can take over its partitions. When this happens,\\n145:  * you may see an offset commit failure (as indicated by a {@link CommitFailedException} thrown from a call to {@link #commitSync()}).\\n146:  * This is a safety mechanism which guarantees that only active members of the group are able to commit offsets.\\n147:  * So to stay in the group, you must continue to call poll.\\n148:  * <p>\\n149:  * The consumer provides two configuration settings to control the behavior of the poll loop:\\n150:  * <ol>\\n151:  *     <li><code>max.poll.interval.ms</code>: By increasing the interval between expected polls, you can give\\n152:  *     the consumer more time to handle a batch of records returned from {@link #poll(Duration)}. The drawback\\n153:  *     is that increasing this value may delay a group rebalance since the consumer will only join the rebalance\\n154:  *     inside the call to poll. You can use this setting to bound the time to finish a rebalance, but\\n155:  *     you risk slower progress if the consumer cannot actually call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} often enough.</li>\\n156:  *     <li><code>max.poll.records</code>: Use this setting to limit the total records returned from a single\\n157:  *     call to poll. This can make it easier to predict the maximum that must be handled within each poll\\n158:  *     interval. By tuning this value, you may be able to reduce the poll interval, which will reduce the\\n159:  *     impact of group rebalancing.</li>\\n160:  * </ol>\\n161:  * <p>\\n162:  * For use cases where message processing time varies unpredictably, neither of these options may be sufficient.\\n163:  * The recommended way to handle these cases is to move message processing to another thread, which allows\\n164:  * the consumer to continue calling {@link #poll(Duration) poll} while the processor is still working.\\n165:  * Some care must be taken to ensure that committed offsets do not get ahead of the actual position.\\n166:  * Typically, you must disable automatic commits and manually commit processed offsets for records only after the\\n167:  * thread has finished handling them (depending on the delivery semantics you need).\\n168:  * Note also that you will need to {@link #pause(Collection) pause} the partition so that no new records are received\\n169:  * from poll until after thread has finished handling those previously returned.\\n170:  *\\n171:  * <h3>Usage Examples</h3>\\n172:  * The consumer APIs offer flexibility to cover a variety of consumption use cases. Here are some examples to\\n173:  * demonstrate how to use them.\\n174:  *\\n175:  * <h4>Automatic Offset Committing</h4>\\n176:  * This example demonstrates a simple usage of Kafka's consumer api that relies on automatic offset committing.\\n177:  * <p>\\n178:  * <pre>\\n179:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\\n180:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;bootstrap.servers&quot;, &quot;localhost:9092&quot;);\\n181:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;group.id&quot;, &quot;test&quot;);\\n182:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;enable.auto.commit&quot;, &quot;true&quot;);\\n183:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;auto.commit.interval.ms&quot;, &quot;1000&quot;);\\n184:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;key.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n185:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;value.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n186:  *     KafkaConsumer&lt;String, String&gt; consumer = new KafkaConsumer&lt;&gt;(props);\\n187:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(&quot;foo&quot;, &quot;bar&quot;));\\n188:  *     while (true) {\\n189:  *         ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\\n190:  *         for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : records)\\n191:  *             System.out.printf(&quot;offset = %d, key = %s, value = %s%n&quot;, record.offset(), record.key(), record.value());\\n192:  *     }\\n193:  * </pre>\\n194:  *\\n195:  * The connection to the cluster is bootstrapped by specifying a list of one or more brokers to contact using the\\n196:  * configuration {@code bootstrap.servers}. This list is just used to discover the rest of the brokers in the\\n197:  * cluster and need not be an exhaustive list of servers in the cluster (though you may want to specify more than one in\\n198:  * case there are servers down when the client is connecting).\\n199:  * <p>\\n200:  * Setting {@code enable.auto.commit} means that offsets are committed automatically with a frequency controlled by\\n201:  * the config {@code auto.commit.interval.ms}.\\n202:  * <p>\\n203:  * In this example the consumer is subscribing to the topics <i>foo</i> and <i>bar</i> as part of a group of consumers\\n204:  * called <i>test</i> as configured with {@code group.id}.\\n205:  * <p>\\n206:  * The deserializer settings specify how to turn bytes into objects. For example, by specifying string deserializers, we\\n207:  * are saying that our record's key and value will just be simple strings.\\n208:  *\\n209:  * <h4>Manual Offset Control</h4>\\n210:  *\\n211:  * Instead of relying on the consumer to periodically commit consumed offsets, users can also control when records\\n212:  * should be considered as consumed and hence commit their offsets. This is useful when the consumption of the messages\\n213:  * is coupled with some processing logic and hence a message should not be considered as consumed until it is completed processing.\\n214: \\n215:  * <p>\\n216:  * <pre>\\n217:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\\n218:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;bootstrap.servers&quot;, &quot;localhost:9092&quot;);\\n219:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;group.id&quot;, &quot;test&quot;);\\n220:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;enable.auto.commit&quot;, &quot;false&quot;);\\n221:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;key.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n222:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;value.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n223:  *     KafkaConsumer&lt;String, String&gt; consumer = new KafkaConsumer&lt;&gt;(props);\\n224:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(&quot;foo&quot;, &quot;bar&quot;));\\n225:  *     final int minBatchSize = 200;\\n226:  *     List&lt;ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt;&gt; buffer = new ArrayList&lt;&gt;();\\n227:  *     while (true) {\\n228:  *         ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\\n229:  *         for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : records) {\\n230:  *             buffer.add(record);\\n231:  *         }\\n232:  *         if (buffer.size() &gt;= minBatchSize) {\\n233:  *             insertIntoDb(buffer);\\n234:  *             consumer.commitSync();\\n235:  *             buffer.clear();\\n236:  *         }\\n237:  *     }\\n238:  * </pre>\\n239:  *\\n240:  * In this example we will consume a batch of records and batch them up in memory. When we have enough records\\n241:  * batched, we will insert them into a database. If we allowed offsets to auto commit as in the previous example, records\\n242:  * would be considered consumed after they were returned to the user in {@link #poll(Duration) poll}. It would then be\\n243:  * possible\\n244:  * for our process to fail after batching the records, but before they had been inserted into the database.\\n245:  * <p>\\n246:  * To avoid this, we will manually commit the offsets only after the corresponding records have been inserted into the\\n247:  * database. This gives us exact control of when a record is considered consumed. This raises the opposite possibility:\\n248:  * the process could fail in the interval after the insert into the database but before the commit (even though this\\n249:  * would likely just be a few milliseconds, it is a possibility). In this case the process that took over consumption\\n250:  * would consume from last committed offset and would repeat the insert of the last batch of data. Used in this way\\n251:  * Kafka provides what is often called \\\"at-least-once\\\" delivery guarantees, as each record will likely be delivered one\\n252:  * time but in failure cases could be duplicated.\\n253:  * <p>\\n254:  * <b>Note: Using automatic offset commits can also give you \\\"at-least-once\\\" delivery, but the requirement is that\\n255:  * you must consume all data returned from each call to {@link #poll(Duration)} before any subsequent calls, or before\\n256:  * {@link #close() closing} the consumer. If you fail to do either of these, it is possible for the committed offset\\n257:  * to get ahead of the consumed position, which results in missing records. The advantage of using manual offset\\n258:  * control is that you have direct control over when a record is considered \\\"consumed.\\\"</b>\\n259:  * <p>\\n260:  * The above example uses {@link #commitSync() commitSync} to mark all received records as committed. In some cases\\n261:  * you may wish to have even finer control over which records have been committed by specifying an offset explicitly.\\n262:  * In the example below we commit offset after we finish handling the records in each partition.\\n263:  * <p>\\n264:  * <pre>\\n265:  *     try {\\n266:  *         while(running) {\\n267:  *             ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(Long.MAX_VALUE));\\n268:  *             for (TopicPartition partition : records.partitions()) {\\n269:  *                 List&lt;ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt;&gt; partitionRecords = records.records(partition);\\n270:  *                 for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : partitionRecords) {\\n271:  *                     System.out.println(record.offset() + &quot;: &quot; + record.value());\\n272:  *                 }\\n273:  *                 consumer.commitSync(Collections.singletonMap(partition, records.nextOffsets().get(partition)));\\n274:  *             }\\n275:  *         }\\n276:  *     } finally {\\n277:  *       consumer.close();\\n278:  *     }\\n279:  * </pre>\\n280:  *\\n281:  * <b>Note: The committed offset should always be the offset of the next message that your application will read.</b>\\n282:  * Thus, when calling {@link #commitSync(Map) commitSync(offsets)} you should use {@code nextRecordToBeProcessed.offset()}\\n283:  * or if {@link ConsumerRecords} is exhausted already {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()} instead.\\n284:  * You should also add the leader epoch as commit metadata, which can be obtained from\\n285:  * {@link ConsumerRecord#leaderEpoch()} or {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()}.\\n286:  *\\n287:  * <h4><a name=\\\"manualassignment\\\">Manual Partition Assignment</a></h4>\\n288:  *\\n289:  * In the previous examples, we subscribed to the topics we were interested in and let Kafka dynamically assign a\\n290:  * fair share of the partitions for those topics based on the active consumers in the group. However, in\\n291:  * some cases you may need finer control over the specific partitions that are assigned. For example:\\n292:  * <p>\\n293:  * <ul>\\n294:  * <li>If the process is maintaining some kind of local state associated with that partition (like a\\n295:  * local on-disk key-value store), then it should only get records for the partition it is maintaining on disk.\\n296:  * <li>If the process itself is highly available and will be restarted if it fails (perhaps using a\\n297:  * cluster management framework like YARN, Mesos, or AWS facilities, or as part of a stream processing framework). In\\n298:  * this case there is no need for Kafka to detect the failure and reassign the partition since the consuming process\\n299:  * will be restarted on another machine.\\n300:  * </ul>\\n301: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kafka--0753c489@31d9200189f81283bf60e929ddc38fd68ac034e2/-/blob/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumer.java%3FL100-300\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"80504b46-0e12-4a8a-8464-b4f5e1fcc866","uuid":"1e0bac80-c067-4ca5-bb3f-29fc3302da21","tool_use_result":{"content":"{\"content\":\"100:  * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribe} APIs. Basically if you don't call poll at least as frequently as the configured max interval,\\n144:  * then the client will proactively leave the group so that another consumer can take over its partitions. When this happens,\\n145:  * you may see an offset commit failure (as indicated by a {@link CommitFailedException} thrown from a call to {@link #commitSync()}).\\n146:  * This is a safety mechanism which guarantees that only active members of the group are able to commit offsets.\\n147:  * So to stay in the group, you must continue to call poll.\\n148:  * <p>\\n149:  * The consumer provides two configuration settings to control the behavior of the poll loop:\\n150:  * <ol>\\n151:  *     <li><code>max.poll.interval.ms</code>: By increasing the interval between expected polls, you can give\\n152:  *     the consumer more time to handle a batch of records returned from {@link #poll(Duration)}. The drawback\\n153:  *     is that increasing this value may delay a group rebalance since the consumer will only join the rebalance\\n154:  *     inside the call to poll. You can use this setting to bound the time to finish a rebalance, but\\n155:  *     you risk slower progress if the consumer cannot actually call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} often enough.</li>\\n156:  *     <li><code>max.poll.records</code>: Use this setting to limit the total records returned from a single\\n157:  *     call to poll. This can make it easier to predict the maximum that must be handled within each poll\\n158:  *     interval. By tuning this value, you may be able to reduce the poll interval, which will reduce the\\n159:  *     impact of group rebalancing.</li>\\n160:  * </ol>\\n161:  * <p>\\n162:  * For use cases where message processing time varies unpredictably, neither of these options may be sufficient.\\n163:  * The recommended way to handle these cases is to move message processing to another thread, which allows\\n164:  * the consumer to continue calling {@link #poll(Duration) poll} while the processor is still working.\\n165:  * Some care must be taken to ensure that committed offsets do not get ahead of the actual position.\\n166:  * Typically, you must disable automatic commits and manually commit processed offsets for records only after the\\n167:  * thread has finished handling them (depending on the delivery semantics you need).\\n168:  * Note also that you will need to {@link #pause(Collection) pause} the partition so that no new records are received\\n169:  * from poll until after thread has finished handling those previously returned.\\n170:  *\\n171:  * <h3>Usage Examples</h3>\\n172:  * The consumer APIs offer flexibility to cover a variety of consumption use cases. Here are some examples to\\n173:  * demonstrate how to use them.\\n174:  *\\n175:  * <h4>Automatic Offset Committing</h4>\\n176:  * This example demonstrates a simple usage of Kafka's consumer api that relies on automatic offset committing.\\n177:  * <p>\\n178:  * <pre>\\n179:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\\n180:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;bootstrap.servers&quot;, &quot;localhost:9092&quot;);\\n181:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;group.id&quot;, &quot;test&quot;);\\n182:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;enable.auto.commit&quot;, &quot;true&quot;);\\n183:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;auto.commit.interval.ms&quot;, &quot;1000&quot;);\\n184:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;key.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n185:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;value.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n186:  *     KafkaConsumer&lt;String, String&gt; consumer = new KafkaConsumer&lt;&gt;(props);\\n187:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(&quot;foo&quot;, &quot;bar&quot;));\\n188:  *     while (true) {\\n189:  *         ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\\n190:  *         for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : records)\\n191:  *             System.out.printf(&quot;offset = %d, key = %s, value = %s%n&quot;, record.offset(), record.key(), record.value());\\n192:  *     }\\n193:  * </pre>\\n194:  *\\n195:  * The connection to the cluster is bootstrapped by specifying a list of one or more brokers to contact using the\\n196:  * configuration {@code bootstrap.servers}. This list is just used to discover the rest of the brokers in the\\n197:  * cluster and need not be an exhaustive list of servers in the cluster (though you may want to specify more than one in\\n198:  * case there are servers down when the client is connecting).\\n199:  * <p>\\n200:  * Setting {@code enable.auto.commit} means that offsets are committed automatically with a frequency controlled by\\n201:  * the config {@code auto.commit.interval.ms}.\\n202:  * <p>\\n203:  * In this example the consumer is subscribing to the topics <i>foo</i> and <i>bar</i> as part of a group of consumers\\n204:  * called <i>test</i> as configured with {@code group.id}.\\n205:  * <p>\\n206:  * The deserializer settings specify how to turn bytes into objects. For example, by specifying string deserializers, we\\n207:  * are saying that our record's key and value will just be simple strings.\\n208:  *\\n209:  * <h4>Manual Offset Control</h4>\\n210:  *\\n211:  * Instead of relying on the consumer to periodically commit consumed offsets, users can also control when records\\n212:  * should be considered as consumed and hence commit their offsets. This is useful when the consumption of the messages\\n213:  * is coupled with some processing logic and hence a message should not be considered as consumed until it is completed processing.\\n214: \\n215:  * <p>\\n216:  * <pre>\\n217:  *     Properties props = new Properties();\\n218:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;bootstrap.servers&quot;, &quot;localhost:9092&quot;);\\n219:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;group.id&quot;, &quot;test&quot;);\\n220:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;enable.auto.commit&quot;, &quot;false&quot;);\\n221:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;key.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n222:  *     props.setProperty(&quot;value.deserializer&quot;, &quot;org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer&quot;);\\n223:  *     KafkaConsumer&lt;String, String&gt; consumer = new KafkaConsumer&lt;&gt;(props);\\n224:  *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(&quot;foo&quot;, &quot;bar&quot;));\\n225:  *     final int minBatchSize = 200;\\n226:  *     List&lt;ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt;&gt; buffer = new ArrayList&lt;&gt;();\\n227:  *     while (true) {\\n228:  *         ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\\n229:  *         for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : records) {\\n230:  *             buffer.add(record);\\n231:  *         }\\n232:  *         if (buffer.size() &gt;= minBatchSize) {\\n233:  *             insertIntoDb(buffer);\\n234:  *             consumer.commitSync();\\n235:  *             buffer.clear();\\n236:  *         }\\n237:  *     }\\n238:  * </pre>\\n239:  *\\n240:  * In this example we will consume a batch of records and batch them up in memory. When we have enough records\\n241:  * batched, we will insert them into a database. If we allowed offsets to auto commit as in the previous example, records\\n242:  * would be considered consumed after they were returned to the user in {@link #poll(Duration) poll}. It would then be\\n243:  * possible\\n244:  * for our process to fail after batching the records, but before they had been inserted into the database.\\n245:  * <p>\\n246:  * To avoid this, we will manually commit the offsets only after the corresponding records have been inserted into the\\n247:  * database. This gives us exact control of when a record is considered consumed. This raises the opposite possibility:\\n248:  * the process could fail in the interval after the insert into the database but before the commit (even though this\\n249:  * would likely just be a few milliseconds, it is a possibility). In this case the process that took over consumption\\n250:  * would consume from last committed offset and would repeat the insert of the last batch of data. Used in this way\\n251:  * Kafka provides what is often called \\\"at-least-once\\\" delivery guarantees, as each record will likely be delivered one\\n252:  * time but in failure cases could be duplicated.\\n253:  * <p>\\n254:  * <b>Note: Using automatic offset commits can also give you \\\"at-least-once\\\" delivery, but the requirement is that\\n255:  * you must consume all data returned from each call to {@link #poll(Duration)} before any subsequent calls, or before\\n256:  * {@link #close() closing} the consumer. If you fail to do either of these, it is possible for the committed offset\\n257:  * to get ahead of the consumed position, which results in missing records. The advantage of using manual offset\\n258:  * control is that you have direct control over when a record is considered \\\"consumed.\\\"</b>\\n259:  * <p>\\n260:  * The above example uses {@link #commitSync() commitSync} to mark all received records as committed. In some cases\\n261:  * you may wish to have even finer control over which records have been committed by specifying an offset explicitly.\\n262:  * In the example below we commit offset after we finish handling the records in each partition.\\n263:  * <p>\\n264:  * <pre>\\n265:  *     try {\\n266:  *         while(running) {\\n267:  *             ConsumerRecords&lt;String, String&gt; records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(Long.MAX_VALUE));\\n268:  *             for (TopicPartition partition : records.partitions()) {\\n269:  *                 List&lt;ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt;&gt; partitionRecords = records.records(partition);\\n270:  *                 for (ConsumerRecord&lt;String, String&gt; record : partitionRecords) {\\n271:  *                     System.out.println(record.offset() + &quot;: &quot; + record.value());\\n272:  *                 }\\n273:  *                 consumer.commitSync(Collections.singletonMap(partition, records.nextOffsets().get(partition)));\\n274:  *             }\\n275:  *         }\\n276:  *     } finally {\\n277:  *       consumer.close();\\n278:  *     }\\n279:  * </pre>\\n280:  *\\n281:  * <b>Note: The committed offset should always be the offset of the next message that your application will read.</b>\\n282:  * Thus, when calling {@link #commitSync(Map) commitSync(offsets)} you should use {@code nextRecordToBeProcessed.offset()}\\n283:  * or if {@link ConsumerRecords} is exhausted already {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()} instead.\\n284:  * You should also add the leader epoch as commit metadata, which can be obtained from\\n285:  * {@link ConsumerRecord#leaderEpoch()} or {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()}.\\n286:  *\\n287:  * <h4><a name=\\\"manualassignment\\\">Manual Partition Assignment</a></h4>\\n288:  *\\n289:  * In the previous examples, we subscribed to the topics we were interested in and let Kafka dynamically assign a\\n290:  * fair share of the partitions for those topics based on the active consumers in the group. However, in\\n291:  * some cases you may need finer control over the specific partitions that are assigned. For example:\\n292:  * <p>\\n293:  * <ul>\\n294:  * <li>If the process is maintaining some kind of local state associated with that partition (like a\\n295:  * local on-disk key-value store), then it should only get records for the partition it is maintaining on disk.\\n296:  * <li>If the process itself is highly available and will be restarted if it fails (perhaps using a\\n297:  * cluster management framework like YARN, Mesos, or AWS facilities, or as part of a stream processing framework). In\\n298:  * this case there is no need for Kafka to detect the failure and reassign the partition since the consuming process\\n299:  * will be restarted on another machine.\\n300:  * </ul>\\n301: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kafka--0753c489@31d9200189f81283bf60e929ddc38fd68ac034e2/-/blob/clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumer.java%3FL100-300\"}","structuredContent":{"content":"100:  * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribe} APIs. Kafka will deliver each message in the\n101:  * subscribed topics to one process in each consumer group. This is achieved by balancing the partitions between all\n102:  * members in the consumer group so that each partition is assigned to exactly one consumer in the group. So if there\n103:  * is a topic with four partitions, and a consumer group with two processes, each process would consume from two partitions.\n104:  * <p>\n105:  * Membership in a consumer group is maintained dynamically: if a process fails, the partitions assigned to it will\n106:  * be reassigned to other consumers in the same group. Similarly, if a new consumer joins the group, partitions will be moved\n107:  * from existing consumers to the new one. This is known as <i>rebalancing</i> the group and is discussed in more\n108:  * detail <a href=\"#failuredetection\">below</a>. Group rebalancing is also used when new partitions are added\n109:  * to one of the subscribed topics or when a new topic matching a {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribed regex}\n110:  * is created. The group will automatically detect the new partitions through periodic metadata refreshes and\n111:  * assign them to members of the group.\n112:  * <p>\n113:  * Conceptually you can think of a consumer group as being a single logical subscriber that happens to be made up of\n114:  * multiple processes. As a multi-subscriber system, Kafka naturally supports having any number of consumer groups for a\n115:  * given topic without duplicating data (additional consumers are actually quite cheap).\n116:  * <p>\n117:  * This is a slight generalization of the functionality that is common in messaging systems. To get semantics similar to\n118:  * a queue in a traditional messaging system all processes would be part of a single consumer group and hence record\n119:  * delivery would be balanced over the group like with a queue. Unlike a traditional messaging system, though, you can\n120:  * have multiple such groups. To get semantics similar to pub-sub in a traditional messaging system each process would\n121:  * have its own consumer group, so each process would subscribe to all the records published to the topic.\n122:  * <p>\n123:  * In addition, when group reassignment happens automatically, consumers can be notified through a {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener},\n124:  * which allows them to finish necessary application-level logic such as state cleanup, manual offset\n125:  * commits, etc. See <a href=\"#rebalancecallback\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</a> for more details.\n126:  * <p>\n127:  * It is also possible for the consumer to <a href=\"#manualassignment\">manually assign</a> specific partitions\n128:  * (similar to the older \"simple\" consumer) using {@link #assign(Collection)}. In this case, dynamic partition\n129:  * assignment and consumer group coordination will be disabled.\n130:  *\n131:  * <h3><a name=\"failuredetection\">Detecting Consumer Failures</a></h3>\n132:  *\n133:  * After subscribing to a set of topics, the consumer will automatically join the group when {@link #poll(Duration)} is\n134:  * invoked. The poll API is designed to ensure consumer liveness. As long as you continue to call poll, the consumer\n135:  * will stay in the group and continue to receive messages from the partitions it was assigned. Underneath the covers,\n136:  * the consumer sends periodic heartbeats to the server. If the consumer crashes or is unable to send heartbeats for\n137:  * a duration of {@code session.timeout.ms}, then the consumer will be considered dead and its partitions will\n138:  * be reassigned.\n139:  * <p>\n140:  * It is also possible that the consumer could encounter a \"livelock\" situation where it is continuing\n141:  * to send heartbeats, but no progress is being made. To prevent the consumer from holding onto its partitions\n142:  * indefinitely in this case, we provide a liveness detection mechanism using the {@code max.poll.interval.ms}\n143:  * setting. Basically if you don't call poll at least as frequently as the configured max interval,\n144:  * then the client will proactively leave the group so that another consumer can take over its partitions. When this happens,\n145:  * you may see an offset commit failure (as indicated by a {@link CommitFailedException} thrown from a call to {@link #commitSync()}).\n146:  * This is a safety mechanism which guarantees that only active members of the group are able to commit offsets.\n147:  * So to stay in the group, you must continue to call poll.\n148:  * <p>\n149:  * The consumer provides two configuration settings to control the behavior of the poll loop:\n150:  * <ol>\n151:  *     <li><code>max.poll.interval.ms</code>: By increasing the interval between expected polls, you can give\n152:  *     the consumer more time to handle a batch of records returned from {@link #poll(Duration)}. The drawback\n153:  *     is that increasing this value may delay a group rebalance since the consumer will only join the rebalance\n154:  *     inside the call to poll. You can use this setting to bound the time to finish a rebalance, but\n155:  *     you risk slower progress if the consumer cannot actually call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} often enough.</li>\n156:  *     <li><code>max.poll.records</code>: Use this setting to limit the total records returned from a single\n157:  *     call to poll. This can make it easier to predict the maximum that must be handled within each poll\n158:  *     interval. By tuning this value, you may be able to reduce the poll interval, which will reduce the\n159:  *     impact of group rebalancing.</li>\n160:  * </ol>\n161:  * <p>\n162:  * For use cases where message processing time varies unpredictably, neither of these options may be sufficient.\n163:  * The recommended way to handle these cases is to move message processing to another thread, which allows\n164:  * the consumer to continue calling {@link #poll(Duration) poll} while the processor is still working.\n165:  * Some care must be taken to ensure that committed offsets do not get ahead of the actual position.\n166:  * Typically, you must disable automatic commits and manually commit processed offsets for records only after the\n167:  * thread has finished handling them (depending on the delivery semantics you need).\n168:  * Note also that you will need to {@link #pause(Collection) pause} the partition so that no new records are received\n169:  * from poll until after thread has finished handling those previously returned.\n170:  *\n171:  * <h3>Usage Examples</h3>\n172:  * The consumer APIs offer flexibility to cover a variety of consumption use cases. {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-sonnet-4-6","id":"msg_019C2QB2h64JX5MZMXoB9F2y","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"thinking","thinking":"Based on my research, here's what I've found:\n\n1. **Legacy ConsumerConnector interface implementations**: The old Kafka consumer API (using `kafka.consumer.ConsumerConnector` or `kafka.javaapi.consumer.ConsumerConnector`) is not present in any of the three target repos (kafka--0753c489, flink--0cc95fcc, camel--1006f047). These repos use the newer API exclusively.\n\n2. **New KafkaConsumer API implementations**:\n   - In `github.com/sg-evals/kafka--0753c489`:\n     - `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumer.java` - The main public-facing KafkaConsumer class\n     - `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/internals/ClassicKafkaConsumer.java` - Internal implementation using the classic group protocol (backward-compatible)\n     - `clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/internals/AsyncKafkaConsumer.java` - New async implementation using the next-gen consumer group protocol (KIP-848)\n   - In `github.com/sg-evals/camel--1006f047`, there's a KafkaConsumer component in the camel-kafka module that wraps the Kafka client library.\n\nThe offset management approach has evolved significantly. The old ConsumerConnector relied on ZooKeeper for offset storage and used blocking message streams, while the modern KafkaConsumer stores offsets in Kafka's `__consumer_offsets` topic and offers both automatic and manual commit strategies. The ClassicKafkaConsumer maintains backward compatibility with the classic group protocol through ConsumerCoordinator, whereas AsyncKafkaConsumer implements the newer event-driven architecture from KIP-848.\n\nSince the legacy ConsumerConnector interface isn't actually present in these repositories, I should focus on documenting what's actually there—the ClassicKafkaConsumer as the closest equivalent to the old API and the modern KafkaConsumer implementation. Let me check the Flink repository one more time to see if there are any other Kafka consumer-related files I should new KafkaConsumer API across the three target repositories:\\n\\n## Legacy ConsumerConnector Interface\\n\\nThe legacy `kafka.consumer.ConsumerConnector` (and its Java wrapper `kafka.javaapi.consumer.ConsumerConnector`) was the old high-level consumer API that stored offsets in ZooKeeper and used blocking `KafkaStream` message queues. This interface has been completely removed from the Kafka codebase and is NOT present in any of the three target repos (sg-evals/kafka--0753c489, sg-evals/flink--0cc95fcc, sg-evals/camel--1006f047).\\n\\n## New KafkaConsumer API\\n\\n### sg-evals/kafka--0753c489\\n\\n1. **`clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/KafkaConsumer.java`** — The primary public-facing new KafkaConsumer class (`public class KafkaConsumer<K, V> implements Consumer<K, V>`). It uses a delegate pattern, delegating to either `ClassicKafkaConsumer` or `AsyncKafkaConsumer` based on configuration. This is the replacement for the old ConsumerConnector API.\\n\\n2. **`clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/internals/ClassicKafkaConsumer.java`** — `public class ClassicKafkaConsumer<K, V> implements ConsumerDelegate<K, V>`. Implements the classic (pre-KIP-848) group protocol for backward compatibility. Network I/O happens in the application thread. Explicitly documented as existing \\\"for backward compatibility to allow users to continue to use the classic group protocol (pre-KIP 848).\\\" This is the closest analog to how the old ConsumerConnector operated (synchronous, per-thread I/O).\\n\\n3. **`clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/internals/AsyncKafkaConsumer.java`** — `public class AsyncKafkaConsumer<K, V> implements ConsumerDelegate<K, V>`. Implements the new KIP-848 next-generation consumer group protocol with a dedicated background network thread and event-driven architecture.\\n\\n4. **`clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/internals/ConsumerDelegate.java`** — Interface that both `ClassicKafkaConsumer` and `AsyncKafkaConsumer` implement.\\n\\n5. **`clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/internals/ConsumerDelegateCreator.java`** — Factory class that selects between `ClassicKafkaConsumer` and `AsyncKafkaConsumer` based on `group.protocol` config.\\n\\n6. **`clients/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/clients/consumer/Consumer.java`** — The Consumer interface defining the full new API contract (commitSync, commitAsync, poll, subscribe, etc.).\\n\\n### sg-evals/camel--1006f047\\n\\n7. **`components/camel-kafka/src/main/java/org/apache/camel/component/kafka/KafkaConsumer.java`** — Camel's Kafka consumer component (`public class KafkaConsumer extends DefaultConsumer`). Uses the new `org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer.KafkaConsumer` API internally to consume from Kafka topics within a Camel route.\\n\\n8. **`components/camel-kafka/src/main/java/org/apache/camel/component/kafka/KafkaFetchRecords.java`** — The actual polling loop that manages `org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer.Consumer` lifecycle, commit management, and error handling.\\n\\n## Key Behavioral Differences in Offset Management\\n\\n### Old ConsumerConnector (historical)\\n- **Storage**: Offsets stored in **ZooKeeper** under `/consumers/<group>/offsets/<topic>/<partition>`\\n- **Commit API**: `commitOffsets()` — synchronous, blocked until committed to ZooKeeper\\n- **Automatic commit**: Periodic background thread committed offsets to ZooKeeper\\n- **Subscription model**: Used blocking `KafkaStream` iterators and `createMessageStreams()`\\n- **No explicit `poll()` method** — consumers blocked on iterator.next()\\n\\n### New KafkaConsumer API (ClassicKafkaConsumer / AsyncKafkaConsumer)\\n- **Storage**: Offsets stored in **Kafka itself** in the internal `__consumer_offsets` topic\\n- **Commit API**: `commitSync()` / `commitAsync(OffsetCommitCallback)` — explicit, per-partition granularity\\n- **Automatic commit**: Configurable via `enable.auto.commit=true` with `auto.commit.interval.ms`; commits are triggered only inside `poll()` calls\\n- **Subscription model**: `subscribe(Collection<String>)` + `poll(Duration)` loop — non-blocking, returns `ConsumerRecords<K, V>`\\n- **Manual commit semantics**: Committed offset = next-record-to-read (offset + 1 of last consumed record)\\n- **ClassicKafkaConsumer**: All I/O (heartbeats, offset commits, fetching) happens synchronously in the calling thread during `poll()`\\n- **AsyncKafkaConsumer**: Heartbeats and network I/O happen on a dedicated background thread; offset commit events are queued as `SyncCommitEvent`/`AsyncCommitEvent` objects processed asynchronously\\n\\nThe flink--0cc95fcc repo does not contain a Kafka connector and has no Kafka consumer implementations.\"\n}\n"},"caller":{"type":"direct"}}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":1,"cache_creation_input_tokens":2364,"cache_read_input_tokens":68683,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":2364,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":0},"output_tokens":8,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"},"context_management":null},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"80504b46-0e12-4a8a-8464-b4f5e1fcc866","uuid":"f9f3ebf7-c32f-47b3-b0cb-80d5bb06642b"}
